    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="508a0222b8e44c08b64e0a1abe27c991">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '215ba1dd-76dd-49aa-aa6a-984b440635bb'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                     <p>Vellore Institute of Technology, Vellore, 632014 India</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Kumar/Dhruv">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Major/Dan+Thomas" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Dan Thomas Major</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-name">Dan Thomas Major</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d78105195" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>An innovative approach to electrolyte engineering in carbonate electrolytes is introduced by incorporating high donor number dual anion additives into the conventional electrolyte system (1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> EC:PC). The active engagement of anions in the primary solvation shell effectively hinders the reduction of solvent molecules by reducing the Lowest Unoccupied Molecular Orbital (LUMO) of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complex as compared to the LUMO of pure solvents or Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complex. The participation of anions leads to the formation of a thinner and an inorganic-rich Solid Electrolyte Interphase on the hard carbon anode enhancing Initial Coulombic Efficiency and significantly improving its kinetics. Moreover, the system with dual anion additives exhibits oxidative stability up to 4.5 V, effectively mitigating the undesired side reactions at high voltage operation of the layered sodium nickel manganese oxide cathode. The addition of dual anion additives proves instrumental in suppressing structural degradation and transition metal dissolution during the long cycling performance of the layered oxide cathode in a sodium-ion full cell. The synergistic effects of this dual anion additive added electrolyte on both the anode and the cathode ultimately ensured prolonged cycling of the sodium-ion full cell. The electrolyte engineering approach outlined in this study opens the door to advancing next-generation high-voltage sodium batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202401268-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) hold great promise as the next-generation energy storage system due to their abundance of sodium resources, lower cost, and reduced environmental impact, making them a compelling solution for scalable and sustainable energy storage systems. However, despite their considerable potential, several challenges, such as suboptimal cycling stability in full cells<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0001_R_d78104673e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and inadequate kinetics between electrodes and electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0002_R_d78104673e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> impede the seamless commercialization of NIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The main obstacles hindering the successful commercialization of NIBs, utilizing a hard carbon (HC) anode and a high-voltage cathode, lie in the challenges associated with low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0003_R_d78104673e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Energy">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the formation of an unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the surface of the HC anode<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0004_R_d78104673e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Mater. Chem. A Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) on the high-voltage cathode,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0005_R_d78104673e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. The composition of the SEI layer developed on the HC anode surface predominantly consists of porous organic components, primarily originating from the decomposition of solvents within the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0006_R_d78104673e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This composition adversely influences the chemical and mechanical stability of the SEI layer, thereby impacting the long-term cycling stability of sodium batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0001_R_d78104673e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, an unstable CEI has been identified as a constraint on the high-voltage operation of the cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0005_R_d78104673e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This instability of CEI causes the continuous decomposition of the electrolyte, leading to the dissolution of transition metals in oxide-based cathodes under high-voltage operation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0005_R_d78104673e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, various investigations have presented methodologies to enhance both ICE<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0007_R_d78104673e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano. Mater. Sci.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and methods to extend the cycling performance of sodium full cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0008_R_d78104673e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0010_R_d78104673e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These approaches include the chemical<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0011_R_d78104673e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0012_R_d78104673e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or electrochemical<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0013_R_d78104673e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> presodiation of HC, the incorporation of film-forming additives,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0014_R_d78104673e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the creation of an artificial SEI on the surface of the HC anode,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0015_R_d78104673e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the formation of a stable CEI on the surface of the cathode through electrolyte engineering<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0016_R_d78104673e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the application of protective coatings<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0017_R_d78104673e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Transact. Nonferr. Metals Soc. China">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0018_R_d78104673e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> on both anode and cathode.</p>
         
         <p>Among these methods, manipulating the solvation structure around the Na<sup>+</sup> cation through electrolyte engineering emerges as the most straightforward and efficient method to modulate the interphase formation on both the anode and cathode, subsequently enhancing the ICE and cycling stability of sodium batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0019_R_d78104673e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - A. C. S. Energy Lett">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0020_R_d78104673e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, most of these studies have primarily concentrated on incorporating fluorinated solvents<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0021_R_d78104673e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0022_R_d78104673e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ChemElectroChem">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as an additive or employing ether-based solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0023_R_d78104673e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it is noteworthy that these solvents face limitations in high-voltage and high-temperature battery applications due to concerns related to anodic stability and inadequate thermal stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0024_R_d78104673e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the context of LIB research, it has been shown that the participation of anions in the inner solvation structure can weaken the cation-solvent coordination and thus induce the preferential reduction of anion by enhancing its reduction potential during single electron reduction<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0025_R_d78104673e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ultimately leading to the formation of anion rich interphases in lithium batteries. However, within the realm of NIB research, a notable need exists for studies exploring the use of anions as additives to enhance the extended cycling performance of sodium full cells.</p>
         
         <p>This study focuses on delving into the solvation structure to comprehend the interactions and conformations of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complexes by adding 2 high-donor salt anions as additives. Introducing high-donor salt anions enables a robust binding with Na<sup>+</sup> cations, compelling cations to draw electrons from donor anions and facilitating additive anions' involvement in the reduction process for forming SEI. We have also explored the impact of dual anion additives on controlling the prolonged cycling performance of the high-voltage oxide cathode, focusing on examining their influence on the CEI. Ultimately, we examined the collective effect of enhanced interfaces on the overall cycling performance of the sodium ion full cell. An innovative approach to regulating the inner solvation structure of the carbonate electrolyte has been achieved by the inclusion of dual anion additives (tetrafluoroborate (BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup>) and (bis(trifluoromethane)sulfonimide (TFSI<sup></sup>)) into the conventional carbonate electrolyte system, consisting of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) mixed in a 1:1 volumetric ratio of ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC). We found an enhanced ICE of the HC anode (39% to 78%) while promoting the formation of inorganic-rich SEI and CEI on the surfaces of the HC anode and high voltage Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMNO) oxide-based cathode, respectively. The participation of dual anion additives in the inner solvation shell around the Na<sup>+</sup> cation induces a change in reductive stability by raising the Lowest Unoccupied Molecular Orbital (LUMO) level of pure solvents and Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complex as compared to the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complex, as confirmed by the Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations. The improved oxidative stability was also governed by lowering the HOMO energy level on the introduction of BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> anion and the preferential participation of Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-TFSI<sup></sup> complex formed an inorganic rich (sulfur and nitrogen containing) CEI that suppressed the unwanted side reactions on employing high voltage cathode. The preferential participation, along with the reduction and oxidation of dual anions, suppresses the decomposition of solvents on the surface of the Char1100 HC anode and NMNO cathode, yielding inorganic component-rich (F, S, N, and B) SEI and CEI, respectively confirmed through X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (XPS) and High-Resolution Transition Electron Microscopy (HR-TEM). To gain deeper insights into the precise composition of the SEI on the HC surface, we employed a powerful and highly recommended Time of Flight Secondary Ion Mass Spectroscopy (TOF-SIMS) technique which confirmed the presence of inorganic fragments resulting from anion decomposition as these anions are located near Na<sup>+</sup> cation, as corroborated by Molecular Dynamics (MD) simulations. The inorganic-rich SEI formed on the HC anode not only formed thinner SEI to enhance ICE but also demonstrated improved rate capability attributed to enhanced diffusion kinetics, as validated by the Randles Sevicik's equation, Distribution of Relaxation Time (DRT) and desolvation energy calculations using Free Energy Perturbation (FEP) methods. The inorganic-rich CEI on the surface of the sodium metal nickel manganese oxide (NMNO) cathode was observed to mitigate transition metal dissolution confirmed through Inductive Coupled Plasma-Atomic Emission Spectroscopy (ICP-AES) and reduced structural degradations, contributing to enhanced long-term cycling performance in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Subsequently, a single-layer pouch cell incorporating the Char1100 HC anode and NMNO cathode in a dual anion additive added electrolyte was fabricated. This cell exhibited an initial cycle Coulombic efficiency of 79%, and it demonstrated the ability to deliver an areal capacity of 1.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> at a current rate of 0.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>, retaining 82.6% of its initial discharge capacity even after 200 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202401268-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202401268-sec-0030-title">2.1 Electrochemical Performance of Char1100 HC Half-Cell in Conventional (Conv) and Dual Salt Added (DS) Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>Char1100 HC anode was synthesized according to the procedure outlined in Schematic <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information and characterized as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1AG</a> (Supporting Information). Further details on characterization can be found in the supporting information file. The conventional electrolyte system is 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> EC:PC denoted as Conv and the dual-salt added electrolyte system is 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> EC:PC&nbsp;+&nbsp;0.1&nbsp;M NaTFSI + 0.1&nbsp;M NaBF<sub>4</sub> EC:PC denoted as DS throughout the manuscript. The electrochemical performance of the Char1100 HC anode in Conv and DS electrolytes was investigated to show the effect of anion additives. The cyclic voltammogram profile of the Char1100 HC anode in the Conv electrolyte (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1A</a>) shows 2 major peaks 1 and 0.38&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> in the first cycle, corresponding to the formation of SEI on the surface of HC. The peak at 1 and 0.38&nbsp;V is attributed to the reduction of solvent molecules to form SEI<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0026_R_d78104673e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the irreversible trapping of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the defective sites on the surface of HC,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0027_R_d78104673e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Carbon N Y">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. The CV profile of the Char1100 HC anode showed a shift in the position of the peaks to lower voltages (10.7&nbsp;V and 0.380.36&nbsp;V) in the case of DS electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1B</a>), confirming the change in the reduction pathways on the surface of HC on the addition of dual anion additives. The shift in peak from 1 to 0.7&nbsp;V is likely indicative of the enhanced reductive stability of solvent molecules, attributed to the introduction of highly coordinating BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> and TFSI<sup></sup> anions in the inner-solvation shell of Na-ion in DS electrolyte. This phenomenon is expected to reduce the overall extent of solvent reduction on the surface of the Char1100 HC anode material,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0025_R_d78104673e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thereby homogenously distributing SEI components on the surface of the anode to form a thin and robust SEI. This has been substantiated by comparing the LUMO levels between pure solvent molecules, Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent, and the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complexes. The analysis revealed decreased LUMO energy levels upon introducing anions into the electrolyte system compared to the pure solvent molecules and Na<sup>+</sup> cation coordinated solvent complex (Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC), as shown in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The findings indicate that the Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-anion complexes are likely to act as the primary interphase precursors, undergoing electrochemical reduction at the HC surface prior to Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC. This process results in an SEI with a prominent chemical fingerprint derived from these anion complexes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0028_R_d78104673e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401268-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4dacc1ee-cbe6-49c5-bff6-920592bc0792/aenm202401268-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4dacc1ee-cbe6-49c5-bff6-920592bc0792/aenm202401268-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ad8a8eb4-0d64-4a89-b9af-102d23e154f8/aenm202401268-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4dacc1ee-cbe6-49c5-bff6-920592bc0792/aenm202401268-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401268-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401268" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cyclic voltammogram profile of Char1100 HC anode in A) Conv and B) DS electrolyte at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;sec<sup>1</sup>; C) HOMO-LUMO energy levels for individual solvent and salt molecules of Conv and DS electrolyte; D) HOMO-LUMO energy levels for Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent anion complexes and Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complex; E) Rate capability test for Char1100 HC anode at different current rates in Conv and DS electrolyte; Linear fitting for calculating the value of <i>b</i> from Randles Sevicik's equation in F) Conv and G) DS electrolyte; Radial distribution function to check the solvation structure around Na<sup>+</sup> cation for H) Conv and I) DS electrolyte.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In a mixture of salts and solvents, the anions associated with different Na-based salts can strongly influence the chemical stability of electrolytes in primarily 2 ways: i) the Highest Occupied Molecular Orbital (HOMO) level of anions limits the oxidative stability of electrolyte and ii) the LUMO level of anions facilitate the formation of SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0020_R_d78104673e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Herein, we have investigated the SEI formation in the electrolyte containing the combination of different Na-based salts and solvents; thus, the LUMO level of all the component salts and solvents has been calculated. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1C</a> depicts the frontier orbitals (HOMO and LUMO) of salts NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaBF<sub>4</sub>, and NaTFSI, along with the solvent molecules EC and PC. The LUMO energy level distinctly shows a declination in values from the solvents to salts, which follows the sequence NaBF<sub>4</sub> &lt;&nbsp;NaPF<sub>6</sub>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;NaTFSI&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;EC&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;PC. The lowest LUMO value indicates a preferential reduction of that specific component in the electrolyte. In this case, NaBF<sub>4</sub> has the lowest LUMO value among the other components, approximating its active participation in the SEI layer formation. These observations correspond well with the MD simulation <i>(discussed later)</i>, and the ex-situ XPS analysis, where the radial distribution function estimates the coordination number of BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> in the first solvation shell is higher than the other constituent anions. To account for the role of cation-solvent-anion coordination in SEI formation, we have additionally optimized the LUMO level of each salt in the presence of both solvents, EC and PC. One EC and one PC molecule have been considered with each salt for simplicity. We have assumed an equal contribution from EC and PC, considering that the coordination number of both solvent molecules is nearly identical in the electrolytes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).On introducing anion in the solvents, the LUMO energy levels for the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1D</a>) complex shift to lower values as compared to pure solvent molecules from 2.0&nbsp;7215&nbsp;eV for EC and 2.0&nbsp;5201&nbsp;eV for PC to 2.4759&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup>, 2.5279&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> and-2.4141&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-TFSI<sup></sup> respectively. The LUMO level of the Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC complex was also found to be higher (2.4023&nbsp;eV) than the LUMO energy levels of all Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-anion complexes. This indicates that the Na<sup>+</sup> cation is withdrawing fewer electrons from the solvent molecules (either pure or coordinated) when anions are introduced into the electrolyte system, thereby improving the reductive stability of the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complex. These anions can form strong bonds with the Na<sup>+</sup> cation, supplying electrons to the cation for the reduction reactions taking place on the HC anode surface. Interestingly, on the introduction of anions, the previous sequence of LUMO energy levels, as followed by individual anions in the electrolyte, still persists, i.e., NaBF<sub>4</sub> coordinated with EC and PC (Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup>) tends to reduce first and contribute to SEI formation earlier than other Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complexes. The sequence also predicts the compositions of the multilayer SEI; the inner layer of SEI adjacent to HC primarily consists of inorganic decomposition products arising from anions, which is covered by the outer organic layer of SEI consisting of decomposition products from the solvent molecules. The estimated HOMO-LUMO values for each structure are tabulated in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Similarly, on introducing anions in the pure solvents, the HOMO energy levels of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complex shift toward lower values from 9.59&nbsp;256&nbsp;eV for EC and 9.74&nbsp;168&nbsp;eV for PC to 9.7431&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> and 9.7954&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-BF<sub>4</sub><sup>-</sup> respectively except for Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-TFSI<sup></sup> (8.2994&nbsp;eV) where a shift toward higher value was found. The decrease in the HOMO levels when 2 salt anions (PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> and BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup>) are present in the system is indicative of the improved oxidative stability of the electrolyte. However, the increase in HOMO level on the introduction of TFSI<sup></sup> anion will cause preferential decomposition of the Na<sup>+</sup>-ECPC-TFSI<sup></sup> complex. This will yield CEI rich in S and N containing inorganic components that may further reduce the unwanted side reactions between the cathode and the electrolyte on prolonged cycling. When present in the CEI, these components also help reduce the transition metal dissolution, as proved through XPS and ICP-AES analysis in the later section of the discussion. The role of NaTFSI salt in reducing vanadium dissolution in sodium vanadium fluorophosphate cathode (NVPF) has already been reported earlier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0029_R_d78104673e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2A,B</a> (Supporting Information) shows the voltage versus capacity (charge-discharge) profile of the Char1100 HC anode in the Conv electrolyte and DS electrolyte at a current rate of 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. The first discharge/charge capacity values for Char1100 HC anode in Conv and DS electrolytes are 641.23/256.61&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> and 403.16/309.90&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. The second discharge/charge capacity values for Char1100 HC anode in Conv and DS electrolytes are 249.65/246.73 and 312.89/303.91&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. The higher second discharge capacity in the DS electrolyte indicates more effective use of reversible sodium storage sites, which are otherwise obstructed by the formation of a thicker SEI and other side reactions in the case of the Conv electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0030_R_d78104673e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Environ. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ICE of the HC anode was found to be 39% and 78% in Conv and DS electrolytes, respectively, reflecting the enhanced ICE in the DS electrolyte. This improvement is attributed to the formation of a thinner SEI with higher inorganic content, enriched with elements like fluorine (F), boron (B), sulfur (S), and nitrogen (N) from the reduction of dual salt additive anions as discussed previously. In contrast, as there is a major participation of solvent species around Na<sup>+</sup> in the primary solvation shell in the case of Conv electrolyte, the solvent species dominantly engage in the initial reduction process to form SEI rich in organic components. We also examined molar ratios of 0.05:0.05, 0.1:0.1, 0.15:0.15, and 0.2:0.2 to evaluate the impact of anion additive concentration on HC electrode performance. Detailed charge-discharge profiles of the HC anode under these ratios are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3AD</a> (Supporting Information). Our investigations revealed a reduction in electrolyte ionic conductivity with increasing salt anion additive concentration, from 8.641&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> for 0.05:0.05 to 7.962&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> for 0.2:0.2 molar ratio of additives. While there was a marginal enhancement in the ICE of HC, from 78% to 81.4%, the resultant decline in electrolyte ionic conductivity notably elevated the total impedance of the cell, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3EH</a>. This phenomenon can adversely affect the long-term cycling performance of the HC anode.</p>
            
            <p>We further found that the formation of thinner and better interphase on the Char1100 HC anode in the DS electrolyte subsequently leads to a better rate capability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1E</a>), providing a capacity of 157&nbsp;mAh g<sup>1</sup> at a current rate of 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> as compared to a capacity of only 69&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> in the case of Conv electrolyte respectively. While there was a minor decline in the charge-discharge capacity of the Char1100 HC anode when transitioning from 20 to 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> in the Conv electrolyte compared to the DS electrolyte, a significant decrease occurred at higher current rates (150 and 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>) in the Conv electrolyte. This indicates that the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions through the SEI is slower for the Char1100 HC anode due to the formation of a thicker SEI in the Conv electrolyte. The average discharge capacity for Conv electrolyte was 286.30&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.6), 258.48&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.69), 205.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.43), 138.66&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.12), and 70.28&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.21) at a current rate of 20, 50, 100, 150, and 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>. The average discharge capacity for DS electrolyte was 334.66&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.02), 298.32&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.05), 254.3 Ah g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.14), 203.03&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD  0.05), and 156.55&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (SD0.04) at a current rate of 20, 50, 100, 150, and 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>. The improved rate capability was further confirmed by the higher value of b in DS electrolyte obtained from linear fitting through Randles Sevicik's equation, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1F,G</a>. The details of the calculation are provided in (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). A higher b value for either peak at a low voltage plateau (0.01&nbsp;V) or a sloping voltage (0.5&nbsp;V) for the DS electrolyte signifies a surface-controlled reaction within the DS electrolyte, contributing to enhanced kinetics for the HC anode. In contrast, a lower <i>b</i> value (0.31 for the peak at 0.01&nbsp;V and 0.70 for the peak at 0.5&nbsp;V) in the Conv electrolyte, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1F,G</a>, suggests a less favorable kinetics for the Char1100 HC anode. Based on the results above, we can infer that the Char1100 HC anode, cycled in the DS electrolyte, exhibits enhanced ICE, greater reversibility, and improved rate capability compared to a conventional electrolyte. MD simulations were conducted to explore further the influence of solvation structure on the degradation of electrolyte components at the anode's surface and to understand the underlying reasons for the improved electrochemical performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202401268-sec-0040-title">2.2 Investigation of the Solvation Structure through Molecular Dynamics (MD) Simulations and Spectroscopy</h3>
            
            <p>The solvation structure of electrolytes is usually examined through a combination of experimental and computational techniques.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0031_R_d78104673e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a>-<a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0038_R_d78104673e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Phys. Chem. B">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We have also employed MD simulations along with Raman and Fourier Transform Infrared (FTIR) spectroscopies to investigate the solvation structure around Na<sup>+</sup> cation in Conv and DS electrolytes. The solvation structure around the Na<sup>+</sup> cation was investigated through MD simulations after equilibration under constant NPT conditions. The final snapshots of the solvation structure around Na<sup>+</sup> cation within a radius of 5&nbsp; obtained through MD simulations in Conv and DS electrolyte are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5A,B</a> (Supporting Information). Analysis of the radial distribution function (RDF) reveals that EC and PC exhibit reduced coordination numbers in DS electrolytes compared to Conv electrolytes, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1H,I</a>, respectively. Moreover, the PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> anion exhibits a low coordination number of 0.005 in the Conv electrolyte but a slightly higher value of 0.015 in the DS electrolyte, indicating enhanced primary anion participation in introducing additives. Like the primary salt anion (PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup>), the dual salt additive anions (BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> and TFSI<sup></sup>) were also found to participate in the primary solvation shell in DS electrolyte, showing RDF values of 2.10 and 2.14&nbsp;, respectively. The predominant involvement of the BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> anion in the primary solvation shell, revealed by its highest coordination number of 0.026 among all the anions, fosters a robust interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup>, culminating in the generation of Na<sub>x</sub>BF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub> and NaF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0039_R_d78104673e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ECS Trans.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The coordination number of each salt, solvent, and additive molecule for Conv and DS electrolyte is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), indicating the highest participation of BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> anion after EC and PC in the DS electrolyte, whereas the preferential involvement of only solvent molecules in the case of Conv electrolyte. Further, the detection of Na<span class="icomoon"></span>F and B<span class="icomoon"></span>F bonds in XPS spectra confirms the role of BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> anion in forming SEI, as discussed in the subsequent section. Therefore, the final solvation structure around Na<sup>+</sup> cation for Conv and DS electrolyte can be shown through the Schematic shown in Schematic <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Further, the involvement of dual anion additives can be ascribed to the higher donor number of BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> and TFSI<sup></sup> anions in comparison to PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> anion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0040_R_d78104673e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This characteristic assists in disrupting the robust interaction between the solvent and Na<sup>+</sup> cation, as can be confirmed through reduced solvent coordination number in DS electrolyte, consequently mitigating solvent reduction during the initial galvanostatic discharge. The involvement of dual anions in the primary solvation shell induces the formation of inorganic components-rich interphases on both the anode and cathode, ensuring the establishment of stable interphases for prolonged cycling stability in both half cells and full cells when employing the DS electrolyte system.</p>
            
            <p>The Raman peak at a wavelength of 715 and 722&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> corresponds to the free EC molecules,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0041_R_d78104673e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Electrochim. Acta">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0042_R_d78104673e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Nano">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and EC coordinated to Na<sup>+</sup> cation does not change much on the addition of dual anion additives in DS electrolyte. However, a sharp narrowing of peak shape (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6A,B</a>, Supporting Information) can be observed when adding dual anion additives in the electrolyte, indicating a decrease in interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and EC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0043_R_d78104673e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A peak at 740&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> was observed, indicating the presence of free PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> molecules in the Conv electrolyte. In the case of DS electrolyte, the peak corresponds to a combination of free PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> and TFSI<sup></sup> anions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0042_R_d78104673e1063" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Nano">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak intensity for the coordination of Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> or TFSI<sup></sup> anions at 744&nbsp;cm<sup>1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0044_R_d78104673e1076" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> increases on adding dual anion additives in the electrolyte indicative of the increased anion participation in DS electrolyte.</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7A</a> (Supporting Information) shows the comparison of FTIR spectra of the mixture of EC:PC (denoted as EC:PC in Figuregreen), EC:PC + NaTFSI (denoted as TFSI<sup></sup> in Figure  blue), and EC:PC + NaBF<sub>4</sub> (denoted as BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> in FigurePink). Apart from the peaks that are present in the FTIR spectra of EC:PC mixture, TFSI<sup></sup> and BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> spectra consist of some extra peaks that are being denoted to free BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> at 528.1&nbsp;cm,<sup>1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0045_R_d78104673e1103" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Phys. Chem. C">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> free TFSI<sup></sup> at 513.9 and 613.4&nbsp;cm,<sup>1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0046_R_d78104673e1111" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. U.S.A.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated to BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> at 1698.6&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated to TFSI<sup></sup> at 1347.5&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> respectively. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7B</a> (Supporting Information) shows the comparison of FTIR spectra of the mixture of EC:PC (denoted as EC:PC in Figure  green), DS (black), and Conv (red) electrolytes. The presence of the free BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> (515.8&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup>), TFSI<sup></sup> (620.7&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup>), PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> (1014.2 and 1282.3&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0046_R_d78104673e1149" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. U.S.A.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and their coordination with Na<sup>+</sup> cation (Na<sup>+</sup>-BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> at 1357.5&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>-TFSI<sup></sup> at 1692.7&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup>) were assigned taking the reference of the FTIR spectra where single anions were being mixed with EC and PC.</p>
            
            <p>From Raman and FTIR spectra, we can conclude that on adding dual salt additives in the Conv electrode, both BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> and TFSI<sup></sup> anions are participating in the solvation around Na<sup>+</sup> along with the solvation from PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> supporting our MD simulation results.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202401268-sec-0050-title">2.3 Study on the Composition and Microstructure of SEI</h3>
            
            <p>The microstructure and chemical composition of SEI formed on the surface of the Char1100 HC electrode recovered after a hundred charge-discharge cycles in Conv and DS electrolyte was investigated through XPS and HR-TEM combined with High Angle Annular Dark-Field Scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) imaging. For compositional analysis, F 1s XPS spectra of HC electrode cycled in Conv electrolyte (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2A</a>) show 2 peaks at 688.2 and 685.5&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the formation of Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0047_R_d78104673e1209" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. A Eur. J.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and NaF,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0015_R_d78104673e1214" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. Similar components of SEI are confirmed through the P 2p XPS, showing peaks at 137.7&nbsp;eV for Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0048_R_d78104673e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Energy">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 134.03&nbsp;eV for Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0049_R_d78104673e1235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2B</a>. It is interesting to note that the ratio of NaF to Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub> in the case of Conv electrolyte is much lesser as compared to the DS electrolyte, where the amount of NaF in the SEI composition is much higher because of the decomposition of the combination of dual additive anions (BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> and TFSI<sup></sup>) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2C,D</a> respectively. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8B,E</a> (Supporting Information) show the C 1s XPS spectra of the HC electrode in Conv and DS electrolytes, respectively. The preferential reduction of EC and PC solvents in Conv electrolyte leads to many organic components such as carbonates, sodium alkoxides, and sodium alkyl carbonates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0050_R_d78104673e1266" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ChemElectroChem">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the preferential reduction of dual additive anions in the case of DS electrolyte leads to the formation of inorganic components such as NaF (685&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0015_R_d78104673e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub> (134.6&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0049_R_d78104673e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>x</sub>BF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub> (192.02&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0051_R_d78104673e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nano Energy">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>x</sub>S<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub> (169.7&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0048_R_d78104673e1307" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Energy">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>x</sub>S<sub>y</sub>N<sub>z</sub>O<sub>z</sub> (400.4&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0048_R_d78104673e1321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Energy">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of nitrogen, boron, and sulfur inorganic components in addition to fluorine and phosphorous compounds in the SEI is further confirmed through N 1s, B 1s, and S 2p XPS of the HC electrode as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2EG</a>, respectively. The estimated reduction pathways for forming these organic and inorganic components are mentioned in Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1S9</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401268-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b93f4908-5e31-4cff-8d57-bd9b0243cef1/aenm202401268-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b93f4908-5e31-4cff-8d57-bd9b0243cef1/aenm202401268-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1f7a41f6-67cb-41da-a85e-14af1e10ccc9/aenm202401268-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b93f4908-5e31-4cff-8d57-bd9b0243cef1/aenm202401268-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401268-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401268" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Chemical composition of SEI formed on the surface of Char1100 HC through A) F 1s and B) P 2p XPS for Conv electrolyte; C) F 1s, D) P 2p, E) N 1s, F) B 1s and G) S 2p XPS for DS electrolyte; Structure of SEI formed on the surface of HC through H), and I) TEM image showing the formation of a uniform and stable SEI on the surface of Char1100 HC after 100 cycles; J) HRTEM image showing the presence of inorganic crystallites in SEI; K) SAED pattern and L) HAADF-STEM image taken on the surface of HC cycled in DS electrolyte.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The presence of N, B, and S containing inorganic components has already been found to be advantageous to the SEI reported in the literature. The boron-containing species (B<span class="icomoon"></span>O and B<span class="icomoon"></span>F) with high ionic conductivity are seen as effective in serving as a SEI binder. This function involves tightly bonding both the inorganic components and the organic polymer, ultimately contributing to forming a resilient and flexible SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0052_R_d78104673e1354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small Methods">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Additionally, the presence of sulfur and nitrogen-containing species in the SEI is found to possess a lower diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup>, reduce the interfacial resistance, and boost Na migration kinetics, thereby improving the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of the Char1100 HC anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0053_R_d78104673e1365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - ACS Energy Lett">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To investigate the structure of SEI formed on the surface of the HC anode in Conv and DS electrolytes further, HR-TEM imaging was done along with HAADF-STEM imaging. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2H</a> shows the TEM imaging of an HC particle with a uniform SEI coated on its surface when cycled in DS electrolyte for prolonged cycling (100 cycles). On magnification, a thin and homogenous SEI of thickness 2025&nbsp;nm was found, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2I</a>, in contrast to the thickness of more than 50&nbsp;nm in the case of Conv electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The HR-TEM imaging of SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2I</a>) shows the presence of lattice fringes from some inorganic crystallites. The d-spacing calculated from the lattice fringes obtained from HR-TEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2J</a>) matches with the d-spacing of Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (d&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.58&nbsp;nm), Na<sub>2</sub>O (d&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.16&nbsp;nm), Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (d&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.27&nbsp;nm), and NaF (d&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.30&nbsp;nm).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0006_R_d78104673e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0054_R_d78104673e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Energy Environ. Sci.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar components were indexed in the diffraction pattern taken over the SEI on the surface of the Char1100 HC anode, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2K</a>. Unfortunately, the lattice fringes from the decomposition product from NaTFSI salt could not be identified through HR-TEM imaging. However, such components could potentially be identified with greater clarity and precision using high-resolution and aberration correction TEM instruments, which, unfortunately, were not accessible for our study. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0002">2L</a> shows the HAADF-STEM image of SEI formed on the surface of the Char1100 HC anode in the DS electrolyte. In summary, a thin, homogenous, and inorganic component-rich robust SEI was found on the surface of the Char1100 HC anode because of the preferential reduction of dual salt additives in the DS electrolyte confirmed through XPS and HR-TEM techniques.</p>
            
            <p>On the contrary, SEI on the surface of the Char1100 HC anode was observed to be thicker and non-uniform in the Conv electrolyte. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9A,B</a> (Supporting Information) display TEM images of the SEI formed on the Char1100 HC anode in the Conv electrolyte, revealing an average thickness exceeding 70&nbsp;nm. The generation of a thicker, non-uniform, and porous SEI was additionally validated through the HAADF-STEM image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9C</a>, Supporting Information). Individual color-coded EDS maps for Na (red), C (green), O (blue), F (purple), and P (pink) are depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9DH</a> (Supporting Information). The mixed color map for the SEI (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9I</a>, Supporting Information) further verifies the prevalence of predominantly organic components on the Char1100 HC anode surface in the Conv electrolyte.</p>
            
            <p>To further confirm the role of dual anion additives, we precisely investigated the composition of SEI formed on the surface of HC employing a powerful TOF-SIMS technique for HC cycled in Conv and DS electrolyte after 10 cycles. We consider 10 charge-discharge cycles to ensure the completion of the reduction or interfacial modification process occurring on the surface of HC during the initial cycles, facilitating the formation of a complete SEI. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3AD</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3EH</a> show the TOF-SIMS spectra in positive secondary ion mode collected from the surface of Char1100 HC anode cycled in Conv and DS electrolyte for 10 cycles respectively. The intensity ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>F<sup>+</sup>/Na<sub>2</sub>OH<sup>+</sup> coming from the decomposition of salt anions is clearly more for the DS electrolyte, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3C</a>. This suggests an increased decomposition of anions during the SEI formation due to the close involvement of anions around the Na<sup>+</sup> cation, as demonstrated by our MD simulation findings. Along with the formation of NaF, we found the presence of decomposition products from NaBF<sub>4</sub> and NaTFSI additives at m/z&nbsp;=&nbsp;72 from Na<sub>2</sub>BO<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3G</a>) and m/z&nbsp;=&nbsp;117 C<sub>7</sub>H<sub>5</sub>N<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3H</a>) respectively, in the mass spectra of HC cycled in DS electrolyte whereas these peaks were completely absent in the mass spectra of HC cycled in Conv electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3C,D</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3IK</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3LN</a> show the TOF-SIMS spectra in negative secondary ion mode collected from the surface of Char1100 HC anode cycled in Conv and DS electrolyte for 10 cycles respectively. The presence of the decomposition products from dual salt anions was also evident at m/z&nbsp;=&nbsp;32 from S<sup></sup>, m/z&nbsp;=&nbsp;&nbsp;37 from NaN<sup></sup>, m/z&nbsp;=&nbsp;48 from SO<sup></sup>, and m/z&nbsp;=&nbsp;49 from BF<sub>2</sub><sup></sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3M</a> which were absent in the mass spectra of HC cycled in Conv electrolyte. All the nitrogen-containing (C<sub>7</sub>H<sub>5</sub>N<sup>+</sup> and NaN<sup></sup>), sulfur-containing (S<sup></sup> and SO<sup></sup>), and boron-containing (Na<sub>2</sub>BO<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup> and BF<sub>2</sub><sup></sup>) decomposition products are marked with red, green, and blue stars in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3</a>, respectively. Apart from the assigned m/z peaks, we found various unknown peaks (marked as black stars) clearly revealing the distinct nature of SEI formed in Conv and DS electrolyte. Therefore, we can summarise that the presence of these inorganic fragments coming from the decomposition of dual anions identified in the TOF-SIMS mass spectra strongly supports our hypothesis regarding the modification of the solvation structure around the Na<sup>+</sup> cation upon adding dual anion additives. The highlights of investigated features of SEI on the surface of HC from the TOF-SIMS analysis are displayed in the box in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0003">3O</a> for Conv and DS electrolytes, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401268-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/839b55d4-13f5-4a88-8a1a-e5d32fd3e66f/aenm202401268-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/839b55d4-13f5-4a88-8a1a-e5d32fd3e66f/aenm202401268-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e729fe9-4f39-417e-a903-46e91c55e197/aenm202401268-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/839b55d4-13f5-4a88-8a1a-e5d32fd3e66f/aenm202401268-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401268-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401268" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TOF-SIMS spectra of positive secondary ion mode for Char1100 HC after 10 cycles in AD) Conv electrolyte and EH) DS electrolyte; TOF-SIMS spectra of negative secondary ion mode for Char1100 HC after 10 cycles in IK) Conv electrolyte and LN) DS electrolyte; O) Characteristic of SEI formed on the surface of HC in Conv and DS electrolyte.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In summary, incorporating dual anion additives has facilitated forming and preserving a thin, homogeneous, and inorganic-rich SEI layer even after prolonged cycling. Consequently, these improvements have enhanced coulombic efficiency, kinetics, cycling stability, and reduced impedance for charge-transfer processes on the HC anode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202401268-sec-0060-title">2.4 Transport Properties of Na<sup>+</sup> Cation in Conv and DS Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>The transport of Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the bulk electrolyte is a critical factor influencing the battery's performance and safety of the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0055_R_d78104673e1559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Electrochim. Acta">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Key battery parameters like capacity, cycling stability, and rate capability are intricately linked to the transport properties of the electrolyte, encompassing factors such as ionic conductivity, ionic mobility, transference number, and the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the bulk electrolyte. In this section, we will explore the correlation between the transport properties of Conv and DS electrolytes and the electrochemical performance of the Char1100 HC anode against sodium.</p>
            
            <p>The ionic conductivity of Conv and DS electrolytes were measured to be 8.893<sup>1</sup> and 8.293&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>1,</sup> respectively. The slight decline in ionic conductivity upon introducing dual anion additives can be attributed to the formation of a bulkier and loose solvation structure defined by a lower total coordination number (5.57) around Na<sup>+</sup> as compared to Conv electrolyte (6.005), characterized by the predominant involvement of the BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> anion in the primary solvation shell. The difference in ionic conductivity values in Conv and DS electrolytes is supported by the Mean Square Displacement (MSD) and self-diffusion coefficients (0.057&nbsp;<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;ns<sup>1</sup> for Conv and 0.050&nbsp;<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;ns<sup>1</sup> for DS) calculated through MD simulations as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0004">4A</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401268-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a0fb8940-3405-4e22-ac00-ad02b29923e6/aenm202401268-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a0fb8940-3405-4e22-ac00-ad02b29923e6/aenm202401268-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eeeacdd2-2882-4e0d-9fb1-51de00ba919e/aenm202401268-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a0fb8940-3405-4e22-ac00-ad02b29923e6/aenm202401268-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401268-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401268" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) Self-diffusion coefficient for bulk Conv and DS electrolyte (inset showing the mean square displacement for Conv and DS electrolyte); Transference number calculation for B) Conv and C) DS electrolyte (insets showing Nyquist impedance plots for Na/Na symmetric cells before and after polarization).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The transference number of Na<sup>+</sup> cation was significantly higher for DS (t<sub>Na+</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.54) compared to the Conv electrolyte (t<sub>Na+</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.36), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0004">4C,B</a> respectively. A high transference number in the DS electrolyte can alleviate concentration polarisation during charge-discharge cycles, leading to increased rate capability,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0056_R_d78104673e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Electrochemistry">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as is evident in the electrochemical performance of the Char1100 HC anode cycled in the DS electrolyte at different current rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1E</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The primary cause of impedance in batteries often arises from the mobility of cations within the bulk electrolyte. The selection of anions significantly influences mobility. The cation mobility is derived from the product of conductivity and the cation transference number, which is higher in the DS electrolyte than the Conv electrolyte, indicating lower impedance processes in the DS system, discussed in more detail in the next section. To further explore the mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the 2 electrolyte systems, we conducted FEP calculations. FEP simulations enable the direct numerical assessment of the free energy of solvation of ions in the electrolytes, offering a deeper understanding of the impact of dual anion additive on Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion in the liquid electrolyte. The charging free energies for a Na<sup>+</sup> ion in the liquid electrolytes are 119.0&nbsp;&nbsp;0.13&nbsp;kcal&nbsp;mol<sup>1</sup> for the Conv electrolyte and 117.8&nbsp;&nbsp;0.15&nbsp;kcal&nbsp;mol<sup>1</sup> for the DS electrolyte, respectively. More negative free energy indicates stronger ion-electrolyte interactions, resulting in increased resistance to Na<sup>+</sup> ion migration across the interphase and, consequently, slower ion diffusion. From the comparison of free energy analysis and the value of b from Randles Sevicik's equation, we may conclude that low (less negative) free energy, higher b value at enhanced scan rates, and good electrode-electrolyte interface are critical features of the DS electrolyte, offering enhanced rate performance in Char1100 HC anode. All the transport properties of Conv and DS electrolytes are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202401268-sec-0070-title">2.5 Distribution of Relaxation Time (DRT) of Char1100 HC in Conv and DS Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>The DRT method enables the differentiation of electrochemical processes with varying time constants derived from electrochemical impedance data. This approach provides a more distinct perspective on the measured spectra, aiding in resolving processes that might go unnoticed using other methods.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The analysis of the DRT was performed to separate the distinct contributions from various processes occurring during the 1<sup>st</sup> discharge and 1<sup>st</sup> charge in the Char1100 HC anode through time domain characteristics. Corresponding to the voltage points indicated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10AD</a>, we have taken Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) during 1<sup>st</sup> discharge and 1<sup>st</sup> charge of the Char1100 HC anode in both Conv and DS electrolytes, respectively. On deconvolution of DRT, we found the presence of 4 distinct processes denoted as P1, P2, P3, and P4, as described in the Schematic shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0005">5A</a>. We have indicated these processes as follows, taking reference from<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0057_R_d78104673e1682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
               
               <ul class="plain-list">
                  
                  <li>P1: Diffusive mass transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> ions through SEI on HC electrode.</li>
                  
                  <li>P2: Charge transfer process through HC electrode.</li>
                  
                  <li>P3: Electron transfer process in the defective carbon to reduce the preadsorbed Na<sup>+</sup> ions.</li>
                  
                  <li>P4: Electron transfer process to deposit Na metal into the HC pores.</li>
                  </ul>
            </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401268-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4195894-b85b-44a5-bba8-f458bc5930ed/aenm202401268-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4195894-b85b-44a5-bba8-f458bc5930ed/aenm202401268-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b97cfa1b-9a69-4994-b967-bb3daae1d0e4/aenm202401268-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4195894-b85b-44a5-bba8-f458bc5930ed/aenm202401268-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
