                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401268-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401268" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) Schematic indicating processes P1, P2, P3, and P4; B) Nyquist impedance plot for Char 1100 HC anode in Conv electrolyte at different voltages (1.1, 0.3, 0.1, and 0.01&nbsp;V) during 1<sup>st</sup> discharge and their corresponding DRT Plots (C, D, E, and F); G) Nyquist impedance plot for Char1100 HC anode in DS electrolyte at different voltages (1.1, 0.3, 0.1, and 0.01&nbsp;V) during 1<sup>st</sup> discharge and their corresponding DRT Plots (H, I, J, and K).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In both electrolytes, process P1 dominates at higher voltages (1.1 and 0.3&nbsp;V) during SEI formation but diminishes toward the end of discharge (0.01&nbsp;V). Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion through SEI is faster in DS electrolytes, indicated by a shorter time constant (Log()) for P1 than in Conv electrolytes. Additionally, the impedance to Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion through SEI is smaller in DS electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0005">5GK</a>) compared to Conv electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0005">5BF</a>), as indicated by the reduced (). These findings align with our previous observations of accelerated kinetics for the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions through HC electrodes in DS electrolytes. The charge transfer process (P2) in the DS electrolyte occurs so rapidly that it cannot be discerned separately from process P1 in the DRT. Conversely, the DRT of the Conv electrolyte indicates a slower charge transfer process, as indicated by the increased Log(). Changing the electrolyte has minimal impact on the impedance of the pore-filling process of HC (P4), except for a slightly reduced impedance for pore filling in the case of DS electrolyte. This observation further aligns with our earlier findings of the sustained plateau capacity due to improved kinetics for pore filling on HC, even at higher current rates in DS electrolytes. However, during 1<sup>st</sup> charge (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11AJ</a>, Supporting Information), all the processes possess lower impedance in the DS electrolyte than in the Conv electrolyte. Additionally, the time constant (Log()) for diffusive mass transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> ions through SEI and charge transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> ions through the Char1100 HC electrode is found to be sluggish for Conv electrolyte as compared to DS electrolyte, which further confirms the role of electrolytes in modulating the various electrochemical processes especially the kinetics of the Char1100 HC anode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202401268-sec-0080-title">2.6 Electrochemical Performance of NMNO Half-Cell in Conv and DS Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>Before exploring the full cell's electrochemical performance, we examined the electrochemical performance of a half-cell consisting of the high-voltage NMNO cathode against Na metal and optimized the long cycling performance of NMNO half-cells taking different molar ratios of dual anion additives as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12AD</a> (Supporting Information). As illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16AD</a>, there is a notable reduction in capacity loss (24.1%15.1%) after 200 cycles as the molar ratios transition from 0.05:0.05 to 0.1:0.1. However, the capacity loss for molar ratios of 0.1:0.1, 0.15:0.15, and 0.2:0.2 are found to be very close to each other which indicates that 0.1:0.1 molar ratio is the optimum ratio for the complete formation of CEI on the surface of NMNO cathode. Then, we evaluated the electrochemical stability window of Conv and DS electrolytes. Interestingly, in CV against carbon-coated aluminum (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6A</a>), DS electrolyte exhibited reduced current after 4.0&nbsp;V (0.772&nbsp;A), while Conv electrolyte recorded 1.18&nbsp;A after 4.0&nbsp;V in the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6B</a>). Even in the 2<sup>nd</sup> cycle, Conv electrolyte maintained a higher current after 4.0&nbsp;V, confirming the superior oxidative stability of DS electrolyte. This enhanced anodic stability in DS electrolytes may be attributed to the inclusion of the NaBF<sub>4</sub> additive, which possesses the lowest HOMO level among the 3 anions present in DS electrolytes (shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0001">1C</a>). After assessing the electrochemical stability window, we checked the performance of high voltage NMNO cathode in Conv and DS electrolytes, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6C,D</a>, respectively. Compared to the Conv electrolyte, an enhanced electrochemical performance was observed in the NMNO cathode with the DS electrolyte at a current rate of 0.1 C. This improvement is attributed to reduced electrolyte degradation at higher voltage and the formation of a stable and thin CEI on the surface of the NMNO cathode. The 1<sup>st</sup> charge/discharge capacities for the NMNO cathode in Conv and DS electrolytes were 129.39/119.64&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> and 136.11/130.88&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. This results in an ICE of 92.46% and 96.1%, respectively, indicating more electrolyte decomposition reactions in Conv electrolyte when operating the NMNO cathode above 4.2&nbsp;V. After 200 cycles, the NMNO cathode exhibited capacity retentions of 84.3% and 62.3% in DS and Conv electrolytes. This result strongly underscores the enduring cycling stability of the high-voltage NMNO cathode when paired with DS electrolyte.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401268-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/035a6b15-480e-46d3-868c-188b9a6f6ab3/aenm202401268-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/035a6b15-480e-46d3-868c-188b9a6f6ab3/aenm202401268-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bf721e13-aa68-45d5-96ac-dff99f0f00f2/aenm202401268-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/035a6b15-480e-46d3-868c-188b9a6f6ab3/aenm202401268-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401268-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401268" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical stability window investigated through CV at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;sec<sup>1</sup> for A) Con and B) DS electrolyte; Galvanostatic profile of NMNO cathode at a current rate of 0.1 C (1 C&nbsp;=&nbsp;173&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>) in C) Conv and D) DS electrolyte; SEM images of E) Pristine NMNO cathode; F) and G) NMNO cycled in Conv electrolyte for 200 cycles; H) NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte for 200 cycles; O 1s XPS on the surface of I) NMNO cycled in Conv electrolyte for 200 cycles; J) NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte for 200 cycles; K) B 1s XPS on the surface of NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte for 100 cycles; L) Percentage of Ni dissolution investigated through ICP-AES on separator extracted from NMNO/Na half-cell cycled in Conv and DS electrolytes after 200 cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To examine the impact of electrolytes on the cycling stability of the NMNO cathode, we analyzed structural alterations of the cathode by acquiring SEM images of the NMNO electrodes extracted from half cells after undergoing 200 cycles in Conv and DS electrolytes. Further, XPS was employed to check the composition of CEI formed on the surface of the NMNO cathode and to detect any possible oxygen evolution in Conv and DS electrolytes. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6EH</a>, SEM images depict Pristine NMNO, NMNO cycled in Conv, and NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte. The Pristine NMNO cathode exhibits a well-defined hexagonal shape (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6E</a>). However, upon cycling in Conv electrolyte, significant cracking, and corrosion of NMNO particles is observed, accompanied by the delamination of the T<sub>m</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layers of the NMNO cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6F,G</a>). In contrast, an intact structure with preserved morphology is evident in the case of DS electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6H</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13AC</a> (Supporting Information) presents the C 1s XPS spectra of pristine NMNO, NMNO cycled in Conv, and NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte. Pristine NMNO exhibits peaks at 284.6&nbsp;eV (C<span class="icomoon"></span>C)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0013_R_d78104673e1846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 285.3&nbsp;eV (C<span class="icomoon"></span>H)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0013_R_d78104673e1851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> from conductive carbon and an additional peak at 289.6&nbsp;eV from the Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0058_R_d78104673e1861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Small">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, NMNO cycled in Conv electrolyte shows peaks at 284.5&nbsp;eV (C<span class="icomoon"></span>C),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0060_R_d78104673e1866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nat. Commun.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 286&nbsp;eV (C<span class="icomoon"></span>O),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0059_R_d78104673e1872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Electrochim. Acta">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 288.7&nbsp;eV (O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0060_R_d78104673e1877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nat. Commun.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte shows peaks at 284.3&nbsp;eV (C<span class="icomoon"></span>C),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0015_R_d78104673e1882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 286&nbsp;eV (C<span class="icomoon"></span>O),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0015_R_d78104673e1888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 288.8&nbsp;eV (O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0061_R_d78104673e1893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Energy Environ. Sci.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 293.3&nbsp;eV from the presence of organofluorine (C<span class="icomoon"></span>F) components<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0062_R_d78104673e1898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Carbon N Y">62</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0063_R_d78104673e1898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Sci. Rep.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> on the NMNO surface. A noticeable increase in C<span class="icomoon"></span>O and O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO components is evident in the Conv electrolyte compared to the DS electrolyte, confirming the greater participation of solvents in the formation of CEI.</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13DF</a> (Supporting Information) displays F 1s XPS spectra, where pristine NMNO shows a peak at 688&nbsp;eV coming from the C<span class="icomoon"></span>F bond from the PVDF binder.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0016_R_d78104673e1909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NMNO cycled in Conv electrolyte exhibits peaks at 685.3<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0015_R_d78104673e1914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 688.2 eV,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0016_R_d78104673e1920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> attributed to Na<span class="icomoon"></span>F and C<span class="icomoon"></span>F bond formation from PVDF, while NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte shows peaks at 685<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0015_R_d78104673e1925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 688.1&nbsp;eV,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0016_R_d78104673e1930" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> corresponding to Na<span class="icomoon"></span>F and C<span class="icomoon"></span>F bond formation from PVDF. The presence of NaF is more pronounced in Conv electrolytes. The structural degradation of the NMNO cathode in the Conv electrolyte is linked to the insulation effect caused by the prevalent presence of NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> species (identified through XPS) on the NMNO surface. These species result from parasitic reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interphase, further causing the dissolution of transition metal upon interaction with the unstable electrolyte. The insulation effect caused by these NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> species induces compositional heterogeneity across the cathode after cycling, leading to stresses, distortions in the metal oxide layer, and fractures in the NMNO particles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0064_R_d78104673e1944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Small Methods">64</a>, <a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0065_R_d78104673e1944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Acc. Chem. Res.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The formation of NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface of the NMNO cathode in the case of DS electrolyte was found to be lesser, as revealed through F 1s XPS. In contrast, excess NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> formed on the NFMO cathode in an unstable Conv electrolyte, causing huge fractures and particle corrosion of the NMNO cathode, impeding its long-cycle performance against sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0064_R_d78104673e1958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Small Methods">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Additionally, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6I</a> reveals O 1s XPS spectra, indicating T<sub>M</sub>-O dissolution at 530.01&nbsp;eV<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0065_R_d78104673e1971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Acc. Chem. Res.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in NMNO cycled in Conv electrolyte, absent in NMNO cycled in DS electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6J</a>). The presence of carbonate (533.54&nbsp;eV)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0066_R_d78104673e1980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Adv. Energy Mater.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and surface-deposited oxygen species (531.99&nbsp;eV)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401268-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401268-bib-0065_R_d78104673e1985" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Acc. Chem. Res.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was also significantly reduced in the DS electrolyte compared to the Conv electrolyte. The presence of additional B<span class="icomoon"></span>O and B<span class="icomoon"></span>F components (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6K</a>) in the CEI on the surface of the NMNO cathode when cycled in DS electrolyte. These bonds are identified as effective in inhibiting undesired side reactions, forming a protective CEI, preserving the structural integrity of the cathode, and ensuring prolonged cycling performance.</p>
            
            <p>A similar XPS analysis was conducted post-cycling to investigate the composition of the SEI on the Char1100 HC anode, which indicates minimal alteration, as demonstrated by XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14AF</a>, Supporting Information). Despite undergoing 200 cycles in the DS electrolyte, the Char1100 HC anode's surface continues to exhibit the presence of inorganic components resulting from the reduction of dual anion additives. This persistence confirms the suppression of the SEI dissolution issue on the HC electrode, attributed to the inclusion of dual anion additives in the electrolyte system.</p>
            
            <p>We additionally performed an ICP-AES analysis of the separator removed from the NMNO/Na half-cell cycled in the Conv and DS electrolytes after 200 cycles to determine the percentage of Nickel (Ni) dissolution in both electrolytes. Our findings revealed a significantly decreased dissolution of transition metal when NMNO was cycled in DS electrolyte (0.09% of Ni), in contrast to Conv electrolyte (0.37% of Ni), as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0006">6L</a>. These findings underscore the role of dual anion additives in mitigating the dissolution of transition metals in the NMNO cathode, thus providing additional support for the enhanced cycling performance observed in the NMNO cathode and Char1100 HC anode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202401268-sec-0090-title">2.7 Electrochemical Performance of NMNO/Char1100 HC Full-Cell</h3>
            
            <p>Following an assessment of the compatibility of DS electrolyte in both the anode (HC) and cathode (NMNO) for long cycle performance as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7A,B</a>, respectively, as well as an investigation into its high voltage stability up to 4.5&nbsp;V, we constructed one side coated single-layered pouch cell incorporating the Char1100 HC anode and NMNO cathode in DS electrolyte with a balanced mass loading of 5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> for HC and 15&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> for NMNO respectively (schematic is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7C</a>). The CV profile of the NMNO/Char1100 HC full cell at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;sec<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7D</a>) exhibits overlapping peaks from the 1<sup>st</sup> to the 5<sup>th</sup> cycle, confirming the reversible storage of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the cathode against the Char1100 HC anode in sodium ion full cell. Except for CV peaks above 3.5&nbsp;V corresponding to Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> redox, we found an additional peak at 3.3&nbsp;V in the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle, which disappeared from the 2<sup>nd</sup> cycle onward. We attribute this peak to the SEI formation on the Char1100 HC anode. The galvanostatic charge-discharge profile of the pouch cell at a current rate of 0.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> yielded a 1<sup>st</sup> charge/discharge capacity of 1.89/1.50&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>, with an excellent ICE of 79% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7E</a>). The formation of the SEI during the first sodiation of Char1100 was also observed at 3.3&nbsp;V in the charge-discharge profile, which has been highlighted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7E</a>. The rate capability of the pouch cell was further investigated by charging and discharging the cell at various current rates (0.25, 0.5, 0.75, 1.00, and 1.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7F</a>. The cell exhibits an excellent rate capability of 0.98&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> even at a high current rate of 1.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>. This suggests that the interfaces developed on both the anode and cathode in the sodium-ion full cell are effectively facilitating the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions across these interfaces.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401268-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f3e8550e-bf05-4d63-9912-dd02fd197534/aenm202401268-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f3e8550e-bf05-4d63-9912-dd02fd197534/aenm202401268-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/395abfbb-0f87-45b1-87ac-650ac17d0dc8/aenm202401268-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f3e8550e-bf05-4d63-9912-dd02fd197534/aenm202401268-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401268-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401268" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Long cycle performance in DS electrolyte for A) Char1100 HC anode and B) NMNO cathode; C) Schematic of one side coated single-layer pouch cell fabricated using Char1100 HC anode and NMNO cathode; D) CV profile of NMNO/Char1100 pouch cell at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;sec<sup>1</sup>; E) Galvanostatic profile of NMNO/Char1100 pouch cell at a current rate of 0.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>; F) Rate performance of NMNO/Char1100 pouch cell at 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, 1.00, and 1.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> respectively; G) Voltage versus time profile of NMNO/Char1100 pouch cell H) Long cycling performance of NMNO/Char1100 pouch cell at a current rate of 0.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2.</sup></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7G</a> illustrates the voltage versus time profile of the NMNO/Char1100 HC full cell. During its initial charge, the cell takes 8.2&nbsp;h, while its discharge lasts 6.1&nbsp;h, both occurring at a current rate of 0.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>. Subsequent cycles show consistent charge and discharge times of 6&nbsp;h, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7G</a>. The pouch cell demonstrated remarkable capacity retention of 82.6% after 200 cycles at a current rate of 0.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>, showcasing outstanding stability for a large-scale format sodium ion battery pouch cell, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401268-fig-0007">7H</a>.</p>
            
            <p>In summary, the remarkable performance of the NMNO/Char1100 HC sodium full cell can be ascribed to several enhancements resulting from the incorporation of dual anion additives. These include improvements in the SEI composition and a reduction in SEI dissolution. Additionally, there are advancements in the electrochemical stability window and an enhancement in the chemical stability of the CEI on the high-voltage NMNO cathode.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0100">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202401268-sec-0100-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, our work introduces an innovative method for regulating the inner solvation structure of carbonate electrolytes by incorporating higher donor number dual anion additives (BF<sub>4</sub><sup></sup> and TFSI<sup></sup>) into the conventional electrolyte system (1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> EC:PC). The inclusion of these additives results in a notable enhancement of ICE in the Char1100 HC anode from 39% to 78%, alongside the promotion of inorganic-rich SEI and CEI on the surface of the Char1100 HC anode and high voltage NMNO oxide-based cathode, respectively. Our findings, supported by DFT and MD simulation calculations, confirm the active participation of dual anion additives in the inner solvation shell around the Na<sup>+</sup> cation, influencing the reductive stability of the pure solvent molecules and Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complex by raising their LUMO levels upon the introduction of anions as compared to the LUMO level of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complexes in the electrolyte system. The improved reductive stability of the solvents was seen in the CV profile of Char1100 HC, where a delayed reduction confirmed by the shifting of the reduction peak at 1.0&nbsp;V to a lower voltage value of 0.7&nbsp;V was observed, which helped in the formation of a homogenous and inorganic components rich anion derived SEI on the surface of Char1100 HC anode. The composition of the derived SEI was revealed by the combination of XPS, HR-TEM, and TOF-SIMS analysis. The presence of inorganic fragments coming from the decomposition of dual anions identified in the TOF-SIMS mass spectra strongly supported our hypothesis regarding the modification of the solvation structure around the Na<sup>+</sup> cation upon adding dual anion additives.</p>
         
         <p>The inorganic-rich SEI on the Char1100 HC anode enhances ICE. It exhibits improved rate capability due to enhanced diffusion kinetics, as confirmed by Randles Sevicik's equation, DRT, and desolvation energy calculations using FEP methods. The inorganic-rich CEI on the NMNO cathode surface mitigates insulative sodium carbonate formation and transition metal dissolution alongside suppressing the structural degradations, contributing to enhanced long-term cycling performance in sodium batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, the single-layered pouch cell, integrating the Char1100 HC anode and NMNO cathode in an electrolyte with dual anion additive added electrolyte, showcased promising characteristics. It exhibited an ICE of 79% and the ability to deliver an areal capacity of 1.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> at a current rate of 0.25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>. Even after 200 cycles, it retained 82.6% of its initial discharge capacity. Our findings emphasize the considerable promise of our novel electrolyte engineering strategy in enhancing both the high voltage capabilities and long-term cycling efficiency of sodium-ion battery systems. This suggests an avenue for further investigation into the electrolyte components to improve the electrochemical performance of these battery systems. The electrolyte engineering method detailed in this study provides a pathway for advancing high-voltage next-generation sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401268-sec-0110">
